راح الغزالى يفتش عن طلبته هذه ، مستعيناً بالعقل ، وبالحواس ، وبظواهر و بما عسى يكون هنالك من فنون أخرى للاستدلال كانت الكتاب والسنة ، معروفة لذلك العهد ، فأحس تضارب هذه الأدلة ، كما حدث هو عن نفسه في كتابه ( جواهر القرآن (۱) ) فقال حاكياً عن قوم ا وتناقضت عندهم ظواهر الأدلة حتى ضلوا وأضلوا ثم قال حاكياً عن نفسه : ( · ولسنا نستبعد ذلك فلقد تعثرنا فى أذيال هذه الضلالات مدة » . وطبيعي أن تتضارب هذه الأدلة ، لأن درجتها من القوة والضعف ، والحق والباطل ، ليست واحدة كان لابد للغزالى أن يولى وجهه شطر هذه الأدلة ليفحصها أولا · ثم يفحص بها ثانياً القلب وقد فحص هذه الأدلة في ضوء العلم اليقينى الذي ينشده والذي : « ينكشف فيه المعلوم انكشافاً لا يبقى معه ريبة ، ولا يقارنه إمكان الغلط ، ولا يتسع لتقدير ذلك ، بل الأمان من الخطأ ينبغى أن يكون مقارناً لليقين مقارنة لو تحدى بإظهار بطلانه مثلا يقلب الحجر ذهباً ، والعصا ثعباناً ، لم يورث ذلك شكا من و إنكاراً ، فإنى إذا علمت أن العشرة أكثر من الثلاثة فلو قال لي قائل : لا ، بل الثلاثة أكثر ، بدليل أنى أقلب هذه العصا ثعباناً ، وقلبها ، وشاهدت ذلك ؛ لم أشك بسببه في معرفتي ، ولم يحصل منه إلا التعجب من كيفية قدرته عليه ، فأما الشك فيما علمته فلا ، ثم علمت أن كل ما لا أعلمه على هذا الوجه ، ولا أتيقنه هذا النوع من اليقين فهو علم لا ثقة به ولا أمان معه ، وكل علم لا أمان معه فليس بعلم يقينى »
Al-Ghazali went on searching for this his demand, aided by reason, the senses, and by apparent meanings and whatever other arts of inference there might be, which were the Book and the Sunna, known at that time. Then he felt the contradiction of these evidences, as he himself narrated in his book Jawahir al-Quran, saying, reporting about a people: 'And the apparent meanings of the evidences contradicted each other until they went astray and led others astray.' Then he said, reporting about himself: 'And we do not consider that unlikely, for we stumbled in the tails of these misguidances for a while.' And it is natural for these evidences to contradict each other, because their degree of strength and weakness, truth and falsehood, is not one. It was necessary for al-Ghazali to turn his face towards these evidences to examine them first, then to examine with them secondly the heart. And he examined these evidences in the light of the certain knowledge he sought, which is: 'The known is disclosed in a disclosure that leaves no doubt accompanying it, nor does the possibility of error accompany it, nor does the mind allow for its estimation; rather, safety from error must accompany certainty in such a way that if one were challenged by showing its falsehood—for example, turning a stone into gold, and a stick into a snake—that would not cause any doubt or denial. For if I know that ten is more than three, and someone says to me: No, rather three is more, on the evidence that I turn this stick into a snake, and he turns it, and I witness that, I would not doubt my knowledge because of it, and nothing would result from it except wonder at the manner of his power over it. As for doubt regarding what I know, no. Then I knew that everything I do not know in this manner and do not have certainty of this type of certainty, it is knowledge with no trust in it and no security with it, and every knowledge that has no security with it is not certain knowledge.'
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage describes al-Ghazali's method of seeking truth and his stringent definition of certain knowledge. The author explains that al-Ghazali began his search using reason, senses, and all available sources of evidence including the Quran and Sunna. He then felt that these evidences contradicted each other, as he himself narrated in his book Jawahir al-Quran, quoting others who said that apparent evidences contradicted so severely that they led people astray, and then admitting that he himself stumbled in such misguidance for a time. The author notes that this contradiction is natural because evidences differ in strength, truth, and falsehood. Consequently, al-Ghazali had to first examine the evidences themselves, then use them to examine the heart. He examined them in light of the certain knowledge he was seeking. Ghazali's definition of certain knowledge is extremely rigorous: it requires that the known be disclosed in such a way that no doubt remains, no possibility of error accompanies it, and the mind cannot even conceive of error. Indeed, safety from error must be inseparable from certainty, such that even if someone were to challenge its falsehood by performing a miracle—like turning a stone to gold or a stick into a snake—it would not cause any doubt or denial. As an example, Ghazali says that if he knows ten is more than three, and someone claims the opposite while miraculously turning a stick into a snake, he would not doubt his knowledge; he would only marvel at the miracle-worker's power. He then concludes that any knowledge he does not have with this level of certainty is not trustworthy or secure, and any knowledge without security is not certain knowledge.

Key Terms
علم يقيني
يقين
انكشاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Certain knowledge is just very strong belief or opinion.
Correction: Ghazali insists that certain knowledge must be absolutely immune to doubt, even in the face of miracles. It is not merely strong conviction but a state where error is inconceivable.

Checkpoint
What example does Ghazali give to illustrate the firmness of certain knowledge?
A) The sun is hot
B) Ten is more than three
C) Fire burns
D) The earth is round

Key Takeaway
For Ghazali, certain knowledge is so firm that even a miracle cannot cast doubt upon it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there any belief you hold that you would still hold even if a miracle seemed to contradict it? If not, does that mean your belief is not certain knowledge as Ghazali defines it? What would it take for you to reach that unshakable certainty?
?
Think of a conviction you currently consider absolutely true. If someone performed a visible miracle to argue against it, would you honestly say you would not doubt? If you would doubt, what does that reveal about the quality of your faith or knowledge? Are you willing to seek the kind of certainty Ghazali demands?
?
You base many of your life decisions on things you think you 'know'. But after reading this, how many of those things would survive the test of a miracle? If none, then where is your ultimate security? Will you begin to pursue the only knowledge that offers true safety from error?

The Analogy of Higher Wakefulness
Ghazali illustrates the possibility of a state beyond ordinary reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses the analogy of waking and dreaming to argue for the possibility of a state of knowledge beyond ordinary reason.

Core Idea
Your waking certainty may be like a dream to a higher state.

In this passage, Ghazali challenges the assumption that ordinary waking reason is the ultimate standard of truth. He proposes a thought experiment: just as dreams seem real until we wake, our waking convictions might seem illusory from a higher state. This lesson unpacks that provocative analogy and its implications for the limits of reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then its translation. Notice the rhetorical question at the beginning and the analogy that follows.
Pay attention to the rhythm of the argument.
2
Identify the core analogy
Find the relationship described: the higher state is to waking as waking is to dreaming. Understand the comparative structure.
3
Consider the two candidates
The passage suggests two possible higher states: the mystical state of the Sufis, and death. Reflect on why each might be considered a 'waking' from the dream of ordinary life.
The Prophet's hadith supports the death interpretation.
4
Reflect on the purpose
Ask yourself why Ghazali would introduce such a possibility. The aim is to unsettle absolute confidence in rational perception and open the door to higher forms of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالإضافة (۱) إلى حالتك التى أنت فيها؟ . لكن يمكن أن تطرأ عليك حالة تكون نسبتها إلى يقظتك كنسبة يقظتك إلى منامك ، وتكون يقظتك نوماً بالإضافة إليها، فإذا وردت تلك الحالة تيقنت أن جميع ما توهمت خيالات لاحاصل لها ؛ ولعل تلك الحالة ما تدعيه الصوفية أنها حالتهم . ولعل تلك الحالة هي الموت قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم الناس نيام فإذا ماتوا انتبهوا .
In addition to the state you are in? But it is possible that a state may occur to you whose relation to your waking state is like the relation of your waking state to your dream state, and your waking state would be sleep in relation to it. Then when that state occurs, you would be certain that all that you imagined are baseless fantasies. Perhaps that state is what the Sufis claim is their state. And perhaps that state is death. The Messenger of God, may God bless him and grant him peace, said: 'People are asleep, then when they die, they wake up.'
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage presents an epistemological analogy to challenge the certainty of ordinary waking reason. It begins by addressing the reader: 'In addition to the state you are in?' – implying that the reader's current state is just one among many. The author then describes a possible higher state: a state whose relation to the waking state is like the waking state's relation to the dream state. In such a state, what we take as real in waking life would appear as illusory as dreams do when we wake up. The author suggests two candidates for such a state: the mystical state claimed by the Sufis, and death, citing the hadith of the Prophet that people are asleep and only wake when they die. The purpose is to undermine the absolute confidence in rational perception, opening the door for a deeper inquiry into truth.

Key Terms
يقظة
منام
صوفية
موت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali is denying the validity of reason entirely.
Correction: He is only pointing out that rational certainty may be limited, not that reason is false.

Checkpoint
What is the relation between the hypothetical higher state and the ordinary waking state?
A) The higher state is unrelated.
B) The higher state is to the waking state as the waking state is to the dream state.
C) The higher state is a dream within the waking state.
D) The higher state is identical to death only.

Key Takeaway
The analogy of waking and dreaming suggests that our rational certainty may be relative to a higher state of awareness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that your current waking life is as relative to a higher state as a dream is to waking, how would your attachment to wealth, status, and relationships change? Are you willing to examine which of your 'certainties' are actually just baseless fantasies?
?
The hadith says, 'People are asleep, then when they die, they wake up.' In what specific ways are you still fast asleep in your daily life—going through motions, ignoring the reality of your mortality? What would it mean for you to wake up before death, even partially?
?
Consider your most deeply held convictions about the world: your career goals, your reputation, your intellectual beliefs. If a higher state were to occur to you right now, which of these would you recognize as baseless? What is the one thing you are most afraid of losing that might be an illusion?

The Two-Month Crisis and Recovery Through Divine Light
Ghazali describes his two-month crisis and recovery by divine light.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali's radical skepticism was resolved not by logical argument but by divine illumination, and why he warns against limiting God's mercy to proofs.

Core Idea
Certainty comes from divine light, not just from proofs.

Ghazali takes us into the heart of his epistemological crisis. After trying and failing to find a proof to resolve his doubts, he fell into a state of sophistry for two months. The cure came unexpectedly—not through argument, but through a light from God. This passage shows the limits of reason and the necessity of divine grace in attaining certainty.

Guided Path
1
Read and feel the crisis
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the emotional and intellectual struggle Ghazali describes.
2
Identify the impasse
Why could Ghazali not construct a proof? He needed primary propositions, but those were in doubt. This created a dead end.
This is a key point about the foundation of reasoning.
3
Mark the resolution
How did the soul recover? Not by arranging proofs, but by a 'light' from God. This light restored trust in rational necessities.
The light is described as the key to most knowledge.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Consider Ghazali's warning: whoever thinks unveiling depends only on proofs has narrowed God's vast mercy. What does this imply about the role of reason?
Reason is not rejected but placed within a larger divine framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلما خطرت لى هذه الخواطر ، وانتقدحت فى النفس ، حاولت لذلك علاجاً فلم يتيسر ، إذ لم يكن دفعه إلا بالدليل ، ولم يمكن نصب دليل إلا من تركيب الأولية ، فإذا لم تكن مسلمة لم يمكن تركيب الدليل، فأعضل الداء ودام قريباً من شهرين أنا فيهما على مذهب السفسطة بحكم الحال لا بحكم النطق والمقال . ثم قال: وعادت النفس إلى الصحة والاعتدال - أى بعد مرض الشهرين - و رجعت الضروريات العقلية مقبولة موثوقاً بها على أمن ويقين ، ولم يكن ذلك بنظم دليل ولا ترتيب كلام ، بل بنور قذفه الله تعالى في الصدر ، وذلك النور هو مفتاح أكثر المعارف، فمن ظن أن الكشف موقوف على الأدلة ، فقد ضيق رحمة الله الواسعة .
When these thoughts occurred to me and were engendered in the soul, I tried to treat them but it was not possible, because their repulsion could only be by proof, and a proof could only be constructed from primary propositions. Since these were not conceded, the proof could not be constructed. So the disease became stubborn and continued for nearly two months, during which I was in the school of sophistry by state, not by spoken word or speech. Then he said: The soul returned to health and balance – that is, after the two-month illness – and the rational necessities returned, accepted and trusted with security and certainty. That was not by arranging a proof or ordering speech, but by a light that God Almighty cast into the chest, and that light is the key to most knowledge. Whoever thinks that unveiling depends on proofs has narrowed the vast mercy of God.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage narrates Ghazali's skeptical crisis and its resolution. It begins by describing his attempt to find a cure for the doubts that had assailed him. The difficulty was that any argument requires foundational premises ('primary propositions', al-awwaliyyat), but these very premises were in doubt. Thus no proof could be constructed, leading to a state of radical skepticism that lasted nearly two months. Ghazali describes this as being 'in the school of sophistry' (safsata) by inner state, not by outward claim. The resolution came not through logical argument but through a 'light' (nur) that God cast into his chest (sadr). This light restored trust in the rational necessities (daruriyyat 'aqliyya) and gave certainty. Ghazali emphasizes that this light is the key to most knowledge, and that those who think unveiling (kashf) depends solely on proofs have limited God's vast mercy. The passage highlights the limits of purely discursive reasoning and the role of divine illumination in achieving certainty.

Key Terms
خواطر
سفسطة
ضروريات عقلية
نور
كشف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali rejected reason entirely and advocated blind faith.
Correction: Ghazali acknowledges the limits of reason when foundational principles are doubted, but shows that reason is restored and validated by divine light. He does not reject reason but places it within a framework of divine grace.

Checkpoint
How did Ghazali describe his state during the two-month crisis?
A) He was a convinced skeptic in both speech and belief.
B) He was a skeptic in inner state but not in outward speech.
C) He continued to believe in rational proofs.
D) He had already found the answer through philosophy.

Key Takeaway
Divine light, not proofs alone, restored Ghazali's certainty and opened the door to knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face deep doubts about your faith, do you rely only on intellectual arguments, or have you ever experienced a moment where clarity came unexpectedly, almost as a gift? How did that change you?
?
Ghazali says whoever thinks unveiling depends solely on proofs has narrowed God's mercy. Have you, in your pursuit of knowledge, inadvertently limited God's ability to guide you through means beyond reason? What would it look like to actively seek that divine light while still honoring your intellect?
?
Imagine yourself in Ghazali's two-month crisis: all rational certainties are shaken. What would it take for you to emerge from that state with renewed trust in your mind? Are you willing to humbly ask for that light, or do you insist on proving everything yourself?

The Four Groups of Seekers After Truth
Ghazali classifies truth-seekers into four groups after his recovery.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's classification of truth-seekers into four groups and his assessment of kalam as insufficient for his quest for certainty.

Core Idea
Ghazali found four groups but no one satisfied his quest for certainty.

After his crisis of skepticism, Ghazali recovered and surveyed the main claimants to truth. He identifies four groups: theologians, esotericists, philosophers, and Sufis. This passage presents his classification and his initial evaluation of kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four groups
Read the passage and note the four groups Ghazali mentions. Each claims a distinct method: opinion, instruction, logic, or direct experience.
Notice the specific Arabic terms Ghazali uses for each group.
2
Understand each group's claim
For each group, understand what they claim to possess (e.g., ra'y, education, burhan, mushahada). This shows the diversity of truth-claims.
3
Examine Ghazali's study of kalam
Ghazali says he studied kalam, mastered it, and even authored works. But he found it 'fulfilling its own purpose' but not his purpose. Why?
Think about what 'its own purpose' might be (defending creed) vs. Ghazali's personal goal (certainty like mathematics).
4
Reflect on the limitation of kalam
The commentary explains that kalam is useful for a Muslim raised in Islam but not for the seeker of absolute certainty. This sets up Ghazali's turn to Sufism.
This is a key moment in Ghazali's intellectual biography.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما شفانى الله من هذا المرض انحصرت أصناف الطالبين عندى - للحق ـ في أربع فرق: ١ - المتكلمون وهم يدعون أنهم أهل الرأى والنظر. ٢ - الباطنية وهم يزعمون أنهم أصحاب التعليم، والمخصوصون بالاقتباس من الإمام المعصوم. ٣ - الفلاسفة وهم يزعمون أنهم أهل المنطق والبرهان. ٤ - الصوفية وهم يدعون أنهم خواص الحضرة ، وأهل المشاهدة والمكاشفة. ثم إنى ابتدأت بعلم الكلام فحصلته وطالعت كتب المحققين منهم، وصنفت فيه ما أردت أن أصنف ، فصادفته علماً وافياً بمقصوده ، غير واف بمقصودی.
When God cured me of this illness, the categories of seekers of the truth were confined for me to four groups: 1) The theologians (mutakallimun), who claim to be people of opinion and speculation. 2) The esotericists (batiniyya), who claim to be people of instruction, distinguished by learning from the infallible Imam. 3) The philosophers (falasifa), who claim to be people of logic and demonstration. 4) The Sufis, who claim to be the elite of the Divine Presence, people of witnessing and unveiling. Then I began with the science of kalam, mastered it, read the books of the scholars among them, and authored what I intended to author. I found it a science that fulfilled its own purpose but did not fulfill my purpose.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
After recovering from skepticism, Ghazali identifies four groups that claim to possess truth. The first are the theologians (mutakallimun), who rely on rational opinion and speculation (ra'y wa nazar). The second are the esotericists (batiniyya), who claim to receive knowledge through instruction from an infallible Imam. The third are the philosophers (falasifa), who claim to use logic and demonstration (mantiq wa burhan). The fourth are the Sufis, who claim direct witnessing and unveiling (mushahada wa mukashafa). Ghazali then describes his initial approach: he studied kalam, mastered it, and even authored works in it. However, he concluded that kalam is a science that adequately achieves its own purpose – defending the creed of a Muslim raised in Islam – but it did not achieve Ghazali's personal goal, which was to attain certain knowledge of religious truth as clear as mathematics. This sets the stage for his turn to Sufism.

Key Terms
متكلمون
باطنية
فلاسفة
صوفية
علم الكلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali rejects kalam as completely useless.
Correction: Ghazali acknowledges kalam as a science that fulfills its own purpose (defending creed) but not his personal quest for certainty.

Checkpoint
What was Ghazali's assessment of kalam after studying it?
A) It is completely useless and should be abandoned.
B) It fulfills its own purpose but not Ghazali's purpose.
C) It is superior to philosophy and Sufism.
D) It provides the same certainty as mathematics.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali found that kalam, while useful for defending faith, could not provide the certainty he sought.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have likely learned many things about Islam from books, lectures, and debates. But is your knowledge of God as certain as your knowledge that two plus two equals four? Have you ever felt that your faith, however orthodox, lacks that kind of certainty? What would it take for you to seek that certainty, even if it meant leaving behind your comfortable intellectual positions?
?
Ghazali studied kalam thoroughly and even authored books in it, but ultimately he found it insufficient for his purpose. What is your 'purpose' in learning about Islam? Are you learning to defend your views, to win arguments, or to know God? If you were honest with yourself, which of these motives dominates your study?
?
The four groups each claimed to have the truth: theologians with reason, esotericists with authority, philosophers with logic, and Sufis with experience. You likely identify with one of these approaches. But are you willing to subject your own approach to the same honest scrutiny that Ghazali applied to kalam? Can you admit that your preferred method may not fully satisfy your deepest spiritual need?

Ghazali's Dissatisfaction with Kalam
Ghazali's autobiographical critique of kalam and his advice to researchers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali found kalam insufficient for his intellectual quest and why context matters when interpreting a scholar's work.

Core Idea
Ghazali's personal crisis was not a rejection of theology itself.

In this passage, Ghazali reflects on his own experience with kalam (theology). He acknowledges that others may benefit from it, but for him, it was mixed with imitation and did not nourish his intellect. His advice to researchers is crucial: do not attribute a view to a scholar without understanding the circumstances in which he wrote.

Guided Path
1
Read Ghazali's critique of kalam
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting Ghazali's admission that kalam did not satisfy his purpose.
Notice that he says 'حصولاً مشوباً بالتقليد' — a benefit mixed with imitation.
2
Understand his advice about context
Ghazali advises scholars to consider the author's circumstances when he wrote, e.g., for himself or others. This is a key methodological point.
This is a lesson for researchers: do not take every statement at face value.
3
Reflect on personal vs. universal judgment
Ghazali states he is relating his own state, not denying benefit to others. This shows his modesty and warns against overgeneralization.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن يكون قد حصل ذلك لغيرى ، بل لست أشك في حصوله لطائفة ، لكن حصولا مشوباً بالتقليد في الأمور التي ليست من الأوليات ، والقصد الآن حكاية حالى ، لا الإنكار على من استشفى به ، به ، فإن أدوية الشفاء تختلف باختلاف الداء وكم من دواء ينتفع به مريض ويستضر به آخر » . و واضح من هذا أن الغزالى ألف في علم الكلام ، مع اعترافه بأنه غير واف بمقصوده ، وأن ما يحتويه ليس فيه غذاء عقله ، وطلبة نفسه . أن وهنا يجدر لى أن أزجيها نصيحة خالصة لأولئك الذين يعانون البحث والتفتيش عن آثار العلماء في العصور الخوالي ، فليس يجدر بهم يسندوا الرأى إلى الشخص لمجرد أنه ذكره في كتاب له ، بل ينبغي أن يعرفوا أولا الظروف التي أحاطت بالمؤلف ، حين ألف الكتاب الذى هم بصدد التأريخ له منه، هل ألفه لنفسه ، أو لغيره ، وتحت تأثير أى عامل من العوامل ألفه ؟ ولى الغزالى وجهه بعد ذلك شطر الفلاسفة ، ليجرى عليهم امتحانه هذا ! والفلاسفة هم أولئك القوم الذين يلجؤون إلى العقل في مسالكهم العلمية، تناول الغزالى بحوثهم التى تعرضوا فيها لموضوعات العقيدة ، عله يجد لديهم من فنون المحاولات العقلية ما يقطع بصحة ما ذهبوا إليه بشأنها، فوجدهم قد اختلفوا فيها اختلافاً كبيراً . 1 لقد رد رسطاليس على كل من قبله ، حتى على أستاذه الملقب بأفلاطون الإلهى ؛ ثم اعتذر عن مخالفته أستاذه بأن قال : أفلاطون صديق ، والحق صديق ولكن الحق أصدق منه . وإنما نقلنا هذه الحكاية ، ليعلم أنه لا تثبت ولا إتقان لمذهبهم عندهم ، وأنهم يحكمون بظن وتخمين ، من غير تحقيق و يقين ] وسرعان ما أدرك الغزالى أن مزاولة العقل لهذه المهمة إقحام له فيما لا طاقة له به ، وأن أسلوب العقل فى تفهم الأمور الرياضية ، لا يمكن أن تخضع المسائل الإلهية (1) قال : له ونوضح أن ما شرطوه في صحة مادة القياس في قسم البرهان من المنطق ، وما شرطوه في صورته في كتاب القياس، وما وضعوه من الأوضاع « في أيساغوجي » و قاطيغور ياس» التى هى من أجزاء المنطق ومقدماته لم يتمكنوا من الوفاء بشيء منه في علومهم الإلهية » . لذلك خرج الغزالى بهذه النتيجة أين من يدعى أن براهين الإلهيات - يعنى عند الفلاسفة – قاطعة كبراهين الهندسيات » . وما دامت براهين الإلهيات عند الفلاسفة لا تنتهى في الوضوح إلى الحد الرياضي الذي يشترطه الغزالى فلا بدله من أن ينفض يده منها . وقد ألف الغزالى فى نقدهم وتفنيد آرائهم . وأغلب الظن أن كتاب ( التهافت ) ألف فى هذه الفترة . (1) ولا يلزم من هذا أن ينفض الغزالى يده من العقل ، فلعله يسلمه إلى وسيلة تكون هي التي توصله إلى الحقيقة ، كما حصل له مع منهج المتصوفة . وجه الغزالى وجهه شطر التعليمية الذين يقولون : [ إن العقل لا يؤمن عليه الغلط ، فلا يصح أخذ حقائق الدين عنه ] . وإلى هذا الحكم انتهى الغزالى عند امتحانه للفلاسفة ؛ فهم إذن في هذه النقطة متفقون . عماذا إذن يأخذون قضايا الدين فى ثوبها اليقيني ؟ ! يأخذونها عن الإمام المعصوم الذى يتلقى عن الله بواسطة النبي أحبب بهذا الإمام و بما يأتى عن طريقه . ولكن أين ذلك الإمام ، فتش عنه الغزالى طويلاً فلم يجده ، وتبين أنهم فيه مخدوعون ، وأن هذا الإمام لا حقيقة له في الأعيان ، فعاد أدراجه وكرّ راجعاً ، بعد ما ألف كتباً ضدهم أوجعهم فيها نقداً وتفنيداً كما يقول هو
It may be that this has occurred for others, but I do not doubt that it occurred for a group, but an occurrence mixed with imitation in matters that are not primary. The intention now is to relate my own state, not to deny it to those who sought healing through it, for remedies for healing differ according to the disease, and how many a medicine benefits one patient and harms another. It is clear from this that al-Ghazali wrote in theology (ilm al-kalam), while acknowledging that it did not satisfy his purpose, and that what it contained did not provide nourishment for his intellect and his quest. And here it is fitting for me to offer sincere advice to those who engage in research and investigation of the works of scholars in past ages: it is not fitting for them to attribute an opinion to a person merely because he mentioned it in his book; rather, they should first know the circumstances that surrounded the author when he wrote the book they are studying—did he write it for himself or for others, and under the influence of what factor did he write it? Then al-Ghazali turned his attention to the philosophers to subject them to this test! The philosophers are those people who rely on reason in their scientific paths. Al-Ghazali examined their investigations in which they addressed topics of creed, hoping to find in them forms of rational attempts that would definitively confirm the validity of their conclusions, but he found them to have differed greatly. Aristotle refuted all who came before him, even his teacher nicknamed the divine Plato; then he apologized for disagreeing with his teacher by saying: Plato is a friend, and truth is a friend, but truth is more truthful. We have related this story only that it might be known that there is no stability and no precision in their doctrine according to them, and that they judge by opinion and conjecture without verification and certainty. Soon al-Ghazali realized that engaging reason in this task is an intrusion into what it cannot bear, and that the method of reason in understanding mathematical matters cannot be applied to divine matters. (1) He said: We will clarify that what they stipulated for the validity of the material of syllogism in the section on demonstration from logic, and what they stipulated for its form in the book on syllogism, and what they set down as the categories in Isagoge and Categories, which are parts of logic and its preliminaries, they were unable to fulfill any of it in their divine sciences. Therefore, al-Ghazali reached the conclusion: Where is the one who claims that the proofs of the divine sciences—meaning according to the philosophers—are as certain as the proofs of geometry? As long as the proofs of the divine sciences according to the philosophers do not reach the clarity to the mathematical limit that al-Ghazali stipulated, he had to wash his hands of them. Al-Ghazali wrote in criticism and refutation of their views, and it is most likely that the book The Incoherence (al-Tahafut) was written in this period. (1) This does not necessitate that al-Ghazali washed his hands of reason, for perhaps he would entrust it to a means that would lead him to truth, as happened with him regarding the methodology of the Sufis. Al-Ghazali turned his attention to the Batinites (al-Ta'limiyya) who say: Reason is not immune from error, so it is not correct to take the truths of religion from it. And this judgment was what al-Ghazali concluded upon his examination of the philosophers; so they are in agreement on this point. From where then do they take the issues of religion in their certain form? They take them from the infallible imam who receives from God through the prophet—how beloved is this imam and what comes through his path! But where is that imam? Al-Ghazali searched for him long and did not find him, and it became clear that they were deceived about him, and that this imam has no real existence; so he returned and retreated after having written books against them in which he wounded them with criticism and refutation, as he says.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is a scholarly commentary summarizing Ghazali's intellectual journey. It begins by describing Ghazali's personal dissatisfaction with kalam (theology). He acknowledges that others may have benefited from it, but for him it was mixed with imitation and did not satisfy his intellectual quest. The author then offers advice to researchers: they should not attribute a view to a scholar simply because it appears in his book; they must consider the author's circumstances and audience when writing that book. Ghazali then turned to the philosophers, who rely on reason. He examined their arguments on creedal matters, hoping to find certain proof, but found instead great disagreement among them. The author cites Aristotle's refutation of his predecessors and his saying 'Plato is a friend but truth is a friend...' to show that the philosophers themselves admit lack of certainty. Ghazali realized that reason cannot handle divine matters as it does mathematics. The philosophers' own logical conditions for demonstration (in Isagoge, Categories, etc.) cannot be fulfilled in their divine sciences. Therefore Ghazali concluded that their proofs in theology are not as certain as geometric proofs. Consequently, he rejected their approach and wrote The Incoherence of the Philosophers during this period. A footnote clarifies that this rejection does not mean Ghazali abandoned reason entirely; he later found a proper vehicle for reason in Sufi methodology. Finally, Ghazali turned to the Batinites (esotericists), who hold that reason is fallible and so religious truths must come from an infallible imam. Ghazali agreed with their critique of reason, but then he searched for such an imam and could not find him; the imam had no real existence. Thus he also rejected their doctrine and wrote refutations of them.

Key Terms
علم الكلام
التقليد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does Ghazali say he wrote in kalam despite not finding it satisfying?
A) He was forced by his teachers.
B) He was relating his own experience, not making a universal judgment.
C) He wanted to benefit others even though it didn't help him.
D) He was imitating earlier scholars.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's dissatisfaction with kalam was personal and context-dependent, not a blanket rejection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever attributed an opinion to a scholar without understanding the circumstances under which he wrote? How might this have shaped your religious understanding, and what can you do to correct it?
?
Ghazali admits that a method that benefits many did not nourish his own intellect. Are you willing to admit that a popular religious practice or teaching you follow might not be nourishing your soul? What step will you take to seek what truly nourishes you?
?
If Ghazali could write volumes on kalam while confessing its inadequacy for himself, what does that say about the books you read or the scholars you admire? Are you motivated by intellectual nourishment or by habit? How will you change your approach to learning starting today?

Method of Learning
intermediate
6 min
Ghazali's Critique of the Philosopher
Ghazali's examination and critique of the philosophers' arguments in theology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's central critique that philosophers' proofs in divine matters do not meet the standard of certainty found in mathematics.

Core Idea
Philosophers fail their own criteria for demonstration in theology.

Having left kalam, Ghazali turned to the philosophers, hoping to find certain proofs for creedal matters. Instead, he found disagreement and realized that their logical conditions for demonstration cannot be fulfilled in divine sciences. This led him to conclude that their proofs are not as certain as geometric ones.

Guided Path
1
Read about Ghazali's turn to philosophy
Read the passage where Ghazali examines the philosophers, noting his initial hope and subsequent disappointment.
The passage includes Aristotle's saying: 'Plato is a friend, but truth is more truthful.'
2
Understand the internal disagreement
Ghazali points out that even the philosophers themselves disagree, as Aristotle refuted his predecessors.
This shows that philosophical doctrines lack stability and precision.
3
Identify Ghazali's main argument
Ghazali argues that the philosophers' own logical framework (Isagoge, Categories) cannot be applied to divine matters. Hence their proofs are not certain like geometry.
He says: 'Where is the one who claims that proofs of divine sciences are as certain as geometry?'
4
Notice the footnote about reason
The footnote clarifies that Ghazali did not abandon reason entirely; he later found a proper role for it in Sufism.
This prevents misunderstanding that Ghazali rejected reason outright.

Commentary
This passage is a scholarly commentary summarizing Ghazali's intellectual journey. It begins by describing Ghazali's personal dissatisfaction with kalam (theology). He acknowledges that others may have benefited from it, but for him it was mixed with imitation and did not satisfy his intellectual quest. The author then offers advice to researchers: they should not attribute a view to a scholar simply because it appears in his book; they must consider the author's circumstances and audience when writing that book. Ghazali then turned to the philosophers, who rely on reason. He examined their arguments on creedal matters, hoping to find certain proof, but found instead great disagreement among them. The author cites Aristotle's refutation of his predecessors and his saying 'Plato is a friend but truth is a friend...' to show that the philosophers themselves admit lack of certainty. Ghazali realized that reason cannot handle divine matters as it does mathematics. The philosophers' own logical conditions for demonstration (in Isagoge, Categories, etc.) cannot be fulfilled in their divine sciences. Therefore Ghazali concluded that their proofs in theology are not as certain as geometric proofs. Consequently, he rejected their approach and wrote The Incoherence of the Philosophers during this period. A footnote clarifies that this rejection does not mean Ghazali abandoned reason entirely; he later found a proper vehicle for reason in Sufi methodology. Finally, Ghazali turned to the Batinites (esotericists), who hold that reason is fallible and so religious truths must come from an infallible imam. Ghazali agreed with their critique of reason, but then he searched for such an imam and could not find him; the imam had no real existence. Thus he also rejected their doctrine and wrote refutations of them.

Checkpoint
What standard of certainty did Ghazali demand from the philosophers' proofs for divine matters?
A) That they be based on scripture.
B) That they be as clear as geometric proofs.
C) That they be accepted by all philosophers.
D) That they be based on innate knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's critique of philosophy was that its theologians fail to produce certainty equal to mathematics.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you claim to be certain about something in your faith, have you genuinely tested your reasoning as Ghazali did, or do you assume certainty without examination? What would it take for you to admit that your 'proof' might be uncertain?
?
You rely on your intellect every day, but when it comes to knowing Allah, do you ever feel the limits of your reason? How do you respond when your mind cannot grasp a divine truth — do you accept it humbly or insist on an explanation?
?
The philosophers' disagreement led Ghazali to reject their path. Is there a belief about God that you hold because of a trusted teacher or argument, but have never critically examined? Are you willing to bring it before the standard of truth, even if it means uprooting what is comfortable?

Ghazali's Examination of Batinism
Ghazali's investigation and rejection of Batinite doctrine of the infallible imam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali rejected the Batinite doctrine of an infallible imam despite agreeing with their critique of reason.

Core Idea
Ghazali found the Batinite imam to be a fictional entity.

After his encounter with philosophy, Ghazali turned to the Batinites (esotericists). He agreed with them that reason alone is fallible, but when they proposed that religious truths must come from an infallible imam, he searched for such an imam and concluded that he does not exist. Thus he also rejected their path.

Guided Path
1
Read about Ghazali's turn to Batinism
Read the passage where Ghazali examines the Batinite claim that reason is fallible and an infallible imam is needed.
Note that Ghazali agreed with the critique of reason.
2
Understand the Batinite solution
The Batinites held that certain knowledge comes only from an infallible imam who receives from God via a prophet.
They reason: reason can err, so we need a guarantee.
3
See why Ghazali rejected it
Ghazali searched for such an imam but did not find him. The imam had no real existence. So he rejected the doctrine.
His rejection was based on empirical verification: the imam is not found.

أن يكون قد حصل ذلك لغيرى ، بل لست أشك في حصوله لطائفة ، لكن حصولا مشوباً بالتقليد في الأمور التي ليست من الأوليات ، والقصد الآن حكاية حالى ، لا الإنكار على من استشفى به ، به ، فإن أدوية الشفاء تختلف باختلاف الداء وكم من دواء ينتفع به مريض ويستضر به آخر » . و واضح من هذا أن الغزالى ألف في علم الكلام ، مع اعترافه بأنه غير واف بمقصوده ، وأن ما يحتويه ليس فيه غذاء عقله ، وطلبة نفسه . أن وهنا يجدر لى أن أزجيها نصيحة خالصة لأولئك الذين يعانون البحث والتفتيش عن آثار العلماء في العصور الخوالي ، فليس يجدر بهم يسندوا الرأى إلى الشخص لمجرد أنه ذكره في كتاب له ، بل ينبغي أن يعرفوا أولا الظروف التي أحاطت بالمؤلف ، حين ألف الكتاب الذى هم بصدد التأريخ له منه، هل ألفه لنفسه ، أو لغيره ، وتحت تأثير أى عامل من العوامل ألفه ؟ ولى الغزالى وجهه بعد ذلك شطر الفلاسفة ، ليجرى عليهم امتحانه هذا ! والفلاسفة هم أولئك القوم الذين يلجؤون إلى العقل في مسالكهم العلمية، تناول الغزالى بحوثهم التى تعرضوا فيها لموضوعات العقيدة ، عله يجد لديهم من فنون المحاولات العقلية ما يقطع بصحة ما ذهبوا إليه بشأنها، فوجدهم قد اختلفوا فيها اختلافاً كبيراً . 1 لقد رد رسطاليس على كل من قبله ، حتى على أستاذه الملقب بأفلاطون الإلهى ؛ ثم اعتذر عن مخالفته أستاذه بأن قال : أفلاطون صديق ، والحق صديق ولكن الحق أصدق منه . وإنما نقلنا هذه الحكاية ، ليعلم أنه لا تثبت ولا إتقان لمذهبهم عندهم ، وأنهم يحكمون بظن وتخمين ، من غير تحقيق و يقين ] وسرعان ما أدرك الغزالى أن مزاولة العقل لهذه المهمة إقحام له فيما لا طاقة له به ، وأن أسلوب العقل فى تفهم الأمور الرياضية ، لا يمكن أن تخضع المسائل الإلهية (1) قال : له ونوضح أن ما شرطوه في صحة مادة القياس في قسم البرهان من المنطق ، وما شرطوه في صورته في كتاب القياس، وما وضعوه من الأوضاع « في أيساغوجي » و قاطيغور ياس» التى هى من أجزاء المنطق ومقدماته لم يتمكنوا من الوفاء بشيء منه في علومهم الإلهية » . لذلك خرج الغزالى بهذه النتيجة أين من يدعى أن براهين الإلهيات - يعنى عند الفلاسفة – قاطعة كبراهين الهندسيات » . وما دامت براهين الإلهيات عند الفلاسفة لا تنتهى في الوضوح إلى الحد الرياضي الذي يشترطه الغزالى فلا بدله من أن ينفض يده منها . وقد ألف الغزالى فى نقدهم وتفنيد آرائهم . وأغلب الظن أن كتاب ( التهافت ) ألف فى هذه الفترة . (1) ولا يلزم من هذا أن ينفض الغزالى يده من العقل ، فلعله يسلمه إلى وسيلة تكون هي التي توصله إلى الحقيقة ، كما حصل له مع منهج المتصوفة . وجه الغزالى وجهه شطر التعليمية الذين يقولون : [ إن العقل لا يؤمن عليه الغلط ، فلا يصح أخذ حقائق الدين عنه ] . وإلى هذا الحكم انتهى الغزالى عند امتحانه للفلاسفة ؛ فهم إذن في هذه النقطة متفقون . عماذا إذن يأخذون قضايا الدين فى ثوبها اليقيني ؟ ! يأخذونها عن الإمام المعصوم الذى يتلقى عن الله بواسطة النبي أحبب بهذا الإمام و بما يأتى عن طريقه . ولكن أين ذلك الإمام ، فتش عنه الغزالى طويلاً فلم يجده ، وتبين أنهم فيه مخدوعون ، وأن هذا الإمام لا حقيقة له في الأعيان ، فعاد أدراجه وكرّ راجعاً ، بعد ما ألف كتباً ضدهم أوجعهم فيها نقداً وتفنيداً كما يقول هو
It may be that this has occurred for others, but I do not doubt that it occurred for a group, but an occurrence mixed with imitation in matters that are not primary. The intention now is to relate my own state, not to deny it to those who sought healing through it, for remedies for healing differ according to the disease, and how many a medicine benefits one patient and harms another. It is clear from this that al-Ghazali wrote in theology (ilm al-kalam), while acknowledging that it did not satisfy his purpose, and that what it contained did not provide nourishment for his intellect and his quest. And here it is fitting for me to offer sincere advice to those who engage in research and investigation of the works of scholars in past ages: it is not fitting for them to attribute an opinion to a person merely because he mentioned it in his book; rather, they should first know the circumstances that surrounded the author when he wrote the book they are studying—did he write it for himself or for others, and under the influence of what factor did he write it? Then al-Ghazali turned his attention to the philosophers to subject them to this test! The philosophers are those people who rely on reason in their scientific paths. Al-Ghazali examined their investigations in which they addressed topics of creed, hoping to find in them forms of rational attempts that would definitively confirm the validity of their conclusions, but he found them to have differed greatly. Aristotle refuted all who came before him, even his teacher nicknamed the divine Plato; then he apologized for disagreeing with his teacher by saying: Plato is a friend, and truth is a friend, but truth is more truthful. We have related this story only that it might be known that there is no stability and no precision in their doctrine according to them, and that they judge by opinion and conjecture without verification and certainty. Soon al-Ghazali realized that engaging reason in this task is an intrusion into what it cannot bear, and that the method of reason in understanding mathematical matters cannot be applied to divine matters. (1) He said: We will clarify that what they stipulated for the validity of the material of syllogism in the section on demonstration from logic, and what they stipulated for its form in the book on syllogism, and what they set down as the categories in Isagoge and Categories, which are parts of logic and its preliminaries, they were unable to fulfill any of it in their divine sciences. Therefore, al-Ghazali reached the conclusion: Where is the one who claims that the proofs of the divine sciences—meaning according to the philosophers—are as certain as the proofs of geometry? As long as the proofs of the divine sciences according to the philosophers do not reach the clarity to the mathematical limit that al-Ghazali stipulated, he had to wash his hands of them. Al-Ghazali wrote in criticism and refutation of their views, and it is most likely that the book The Incoherence (al-Tahafut) was written in this period. (1) This does not necessitate that al-Ghazali washed his hands of reason, for perhaps he would entrust it to a means that would lead him to truth, as happened with him regarding the methodology of the Sufis. Al-Ghazali turned his attention to the Batinites (al-Ta'limiyya) who say: Reason is not immune from error, so it is not correct to take the truths of religion from it. And this judgment was what al-Ghazali concluded upon his examination of the philosophers; so they are in agreement on this point. From where then do they take the issues of religion in their certain form? They take them from the infallible imam who receives from God through the prophet—how beloved is this imam and what comes through his path! But where is that imam? Al-Ghazali searched for him long and did not find him, and it became clear that they were deceived about him, and that this imam has no real existence; so he returned and retreated after having written books against them in which he wounded them with criticism and refutation, as he says.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is a scholarly commentary summarizing Ghazali's intellectual journey. It begins by describing Ghazali's personal dissatisfaction with kalam (theology). He acknowledges that others may have benefited from it, but for him it was mixed with imitation and did not satisfy his intellectual quest. The author then offers advice to researchers: they should not attribute a view to a scholar simply because it appears in his book; they must consider the author's circumstances and audience when writing that book. Ghazali then turned to the philosophers, who rely on reason. He examined their arguments on creedal matters, hoping to find certain proof, but found instead great disagreement among them. The author cites Aristotle's refutation of his predecessors and his saying 'Plato is a friend but truth is a friend...' to show that the philosophers themselves admit lack of certainty. Ghazali realized that reason cannot handle divine matters as it does mathematics. The philosophers' own logical conditions for demonstration (in Isagoge, Categories, etc.) cannot be fulfilled in their divine sciences. Therefore Ghazali concluded that their proofs in theology are not as certain as geometric proofs. Consequently, he rejected their approach and wrote The Incoherence of the Philosophers during this period. A footnote clarifies that this rejection does not mean Ghazali abandoned reason entirely; he later found a proper vehicle for reason in Sufi methodology. Finally, Ghazali turned to the Batinites (esotericists), who hold that reason is fallible and so religious truths must come from an infallible imam. Ghazali agreed with their critique of reason, but then he searched for such an imam and could not find him; the imam had no real existence. Thus he also rejected their doctrine and wrote refutations of them.

الإمام المعصوم

Checkpoint
Why did Ghazali reject the Batinite doctrine of an infallible imam?
A) Because he believed reason is infallible.
B) Because he could not find such an imam in reality.
C) Because he preferred philosophy.
D) Because the imam contradicted scripture.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's rejection of Batinism was based on the absence of a verifiable infallible imam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ghazali searched long and hard for the infallible imam and found nothing. Are there religious authorities you follow without demanding evidence? Would your faith survive if you actually examined their claims as rigorously as Ghazali did?
?
If reason is fallible and no infallible guide exists, on what foundation have you built your certainty? Is it blind imitation, or have you personally verified the core truths of your religion?
?
The Batinites were deceived by a pleasing idea—an infallible teacher to save them from doubt. What seemingly attractive beliefs have you embraced that, upon honest scrutiny, might also prove to have no real basis? Will you search them out today?

Ghazali's Critique: Philosophers Fail Their Own Logical Standards
Ghazali states that philosophers fail to meet their own logical standards in metaphysics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Al-Ghazali argued that the philosophers' metaphysical proofs do not meet the logical standards they themselves set for demonstrative syllogisms.

Core Idea
Philosophers' metaphysical proofs fail their own logical test.

In this passage from Tahafut al-Falasifa, Ghazali makes a sharp criticism of the philosophers: they claim their metaphysical proofs are decisive, yet they cannot fulfill the rigorous conditions for a valid demonstrative syllogism that they themselves established in their logical works. By their own standards, their theology falls short.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its English translation. Notice the list of logical requirements Ghazali mentions.
Pay attention to the terms: برهان, مادة القياس, صورة القياس, أيساغوجي, قاطيغورياس.
2
Identify the conditions stated
Ghazali enumerates three areas where philosophers set conditions: matter of the syllogism, form of the syllogism, and the content of Isagoge and Categories. Find these in the text.
3
Understand the failure claim
Ghazali asserts that philosophers were unable to fulfill any of these conditions in their divine sciences (metaphysics). This is the core of his critique.
The failure is not due to lack of effort but because the subject matter itself resists such rigor.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Ghazali ends with a rhetorical question challenging anyone who claims metaphysical proofs are as decisive as geometrical ones. Consider why geometry serves as the model of certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونوضح أن ما شرطوه في صحة مادة القياس في قسم البرهان من المنطق ، وما شرطوه في صورته في كتاب القياس، وما وضعوه من الأوضاع « في أيساغوجي » و قاطيغورياس التى هى من أجزاء المنطق ومقدماته لم يتمكنوا من الوفاء بشيء منه في علومهم الإلهية. لذلك خرج الغزالى بهذه النتيجة: أين من يدعى أن براهين الإلهيات - يعنى عند الفلاسفة – قاطعة كبراهين الهندسيات.
And we clarify that what they stipulated for the validity of the matter of the syllogism in the section of demonstration in logic, and what they stipulated for its form in the book of syllogism, and what they laid down as positions in Isagoge and Categories, which are parts of logic and its preliminaries – they were unable to fulfill any of it in their divine sciences. Therefore, al-Ghazali arrived at this conclusion: Where is the one who claims that the proofs of the divine things – meaning according to the philosophers – are as decisive as the proofs of the geometrical things?
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the philosophers fail to meet their own logical standards in metaphysics. It begins by enumerating the conditions they themselves set for a valid demonstrative syllogism: conditions for the material content (matter) and formal structure (form), as taught in works like Porphyry's Isagoge and Aristotle's Categories. The passage then asserts that the philosophers do not fulfill any of these conditions in their metaphysical sciences. Consequently, al-Ghazali concludes by challenging anyone who claims that the philosophers' proofs in theology are as certain and decisive as those in geometry. The argument follows a logical order: first outlining the required standards, then stating the failure, and finally drawing the conclusion that their metaphysical proofs are not demonstrative.

Key Terms
برهان
مادة القياس
صورة القياس
أيساغوجي
قاطيغورياس
إلهيات
رياضيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali is rejecting all rational proofs in religion.
Correction: Ghazali is only rejecting the philosophers' claim that their metaphysical proofs meet the same rigorous standard as geometrical demonstrations. He does not reject rational proof per se.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did al-Ghazali conclude that the philosophers' metaphysical proofs are not decisive?
A) Because they are not based on revelation.
B) Because they do not fulfill the logical conditions they themselves set for demonstrative proofs.
C) Because they are too difficult to understand.
D) Because they contradict the Quran.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali undermines the philosophers' authority by showing that their own logical standards are not met in metaphysics, so their proofs lack the certainty of geometry.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you claim to have a 'proof' for something in your faith, do you hold yourself to the same standard of certainty as you would in a mathematical or scientific claim? Why or why not?
?
Ghazali exposes the philosophers for not living up to their own standards. In what area of your life are you most vulnerable to claiming certainty without meeting the standards you would require of others?
?
If you discovered that your most cherished belief about Allah or the afterlife was based on a proof that failed its own logical criteria, would you be willing to abandon it? What would that cost you?

Ghazali's Turn to Sufism
Describes Ghazali's adoption of the Sufi path through personal application.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali abandoned his worldly status and turned to the Sufi path based on their claim that unveiling is achieved through knowledge and action.

Core Idea
Ghazali found the Sufi path by abandoning prestige and seeking seclusion.

This lesson introduces the fourth group, the Sufis, who claim direct knowledge through unveiling. Ghazali describes his own journey: he investigated their way, applied it, and ultimately left behind his prestigious position to wander in seclusion. This personal account illustrates the seriousness of his spiritual quest.

Guided Path
1
Read the Sufi claim
Read the first sentence identifying the Sufis and their claim of kashf (unveiling), mu'āyana (direct vision), and connection to the spiritual realm.
Notice the question they pose: what is the way?
2
Follow Ghazali's journey
Read how Ghazali inquired, applied, and eventually abandoned his worldly status and security to travel in seclusion.
His travels to Syria, Hijaz, and Egypt show his total commitment.
3
Reflect on the motivation
Consider why Ghazali fled from people and pursued seclusion. This is directly tied to the Sufi emphasis on cutting attachments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بقيت رابعة الفرق، بقى المتصوفة الذين يقولون بالكشف والمعاينة، والاتصال بعالم الملكوت، والأخذ عنه مباشرة، والاطلاع على اللوح المحفوظ وما يحتويه من أسرار، ولكن ما الطريق إلى الكشف والمعاينة؟، أجابوه بأنها علم وعمل. مضى الغزالى يستوضحهم ويطبق على نفسه حتى أدت الحال إلى أن به ترك هذا الجاه العريض، والشأن المنظوم الخالي عن التكدير والتنغيص، والأمن المسلّم الصافي عن منازعة الخصوم. وخرج هائماً على وجهه إلى الصحارى والقفار، ذاهباً مرة إلى الشام، وأخرى إلى الحجاز، وثالثة إلى مصر. كل ذلك فراراً بنفسه من الناس، وجرياً وراء الخلوة، تطبيقاً لما أشار به عليه الصوفية، الذين يرون أن أساس طريقتهم: قطع علائق القلب عن الدنيا؛ بالتجافى عن دار الغرور، والإنابة إلى دار الخلود، والإقبال بكنه الهمة على الله تعالى، وذلك لا يتم إلا بالإعراض عن الجاه والمال، والهرب من الشواغل والعلائق، بل يصير قلبه إلى حالة يستوى فيها وجود كل شيء وعدمه. ومن تمام طريقتهم أيضاً، كما يقول الغزالي: أن تخلو بنفسك فى زاوية، تقتصر، من العبادة على الفرائض والرواتب وتجلس فارغ القلب، مجموع الهم، مقبلا بذكرك على الله، وذلك في أول الأمر بأن تواظب باللسان على ذكر الله تعالى فلا تزال تقول: الله الله مع حضور القلب وإدراكه، إلى أن تنتهى إلى حالة لو تركت تحريك اللسان لرأيت كأن الكلمة جارية على لسانك لكثرة اعتياده، ثم تصير مواظباً عليه، إلى أن لا يبقى في قلبك إلا معنى اللفظ، ولا يخطر ببالك حروف اللفظ وهيئات الكلمة، بل يبقى المعنى المجرد حاضراً فى قلبك على اللزوم والدوام، ولك اختيار إلى هذا الحد فقط، ولا اختيار بعده لك، إلا في الاستدامة لدفع الوساوس الصارفة، ثم ينقطع اختيارك فلا يبقى لك إلا الانتظار لما يظهر من فتوح ظهر مثله للأولياء، وهو بعض ما يظهر الأنباء. ومنازل أولياء الله فيه لا تحصى.
There remained the fourth group, the Sufis, who speak of unveiling and direct vision, connection with the world of the dominion, taking directly from it, and access to the Preserved Tablet and what it contains of secrets. But what is the way to unveiling and direct vision? They answered that it is knowledge and action. Al-Ghazali proceeded to inquire from them and apply it on himself until the matter led him to abandon this wide prestige, and the orderly state free from trouble and vexation, and the secure safety pure from disputation of opponents. And he went out wandering to deserts and wilderness, going once to Syria, another time to the Hijaz, and a third to Egypt. All that was fleeing with himself from people, and pursuing seclusion, applying what the Sufis indicated to him, they who hold that the foundation of their method is: cutting the heart's attachments to the world; by turning away from the abode of delusion, turning in repentance to the abode of eternity, and directing the whole effort towards God Almighty. And that is not accomplished except by turning away from prestige and wealth, and fleeing from distractions and attachments; rather his heart reaches a state where the existence of everything and its non-existence are equal to him. And from the completeness of their method also, as al-Ghazali says: that you seclude yourself in a corner, limiting worship to the obligatory and the regular supererogatory prayers, and you sit empty-hearted, with concentrated concern, turning with your remembrance towards God. And that in the beginning is by persisting with the tongue in the remembrance of God Almighty, so you keep saying 'Allah, Allah' with presence of heart and awareness, until you reach a state where if you stopped moving your tongue you would see as if the word is flowing on your tongue due to much habit. Then you persist in it, until there remains in your heart only the meaning of the word, and the letters of the word and the forms of the word do not occur to your mind; rather the abstract meaning remains present in your heart continuously and permanently. You have choice only up to this point, and there is no choice for you after that, except in continuing to repel distracting whispers. Then your choice ceases, and there remains for you only waiting for what appears from openings the like of which appeared to the saints, and it is some of what appears to the prophets. And the stations of the saints of God in it are countless.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is a descriptive and instructive account of Ghazali's turn to Sufism and the Sufi method. It begins by identifying the Sufis as the fourth group who claim knowledge through unveiling (kashf) and direct vision (mu'āyana), connection with the spiritual world (malakūt), and access to the Preserved Tablet. The question is posed: what is the way? The answer: knowledge and action. Ghazali then describes his own journey: he inquired, applied the method, and eventually abandoned worldly status and security to travel in seclusion to various lands, applying the Sufi teachings. The foundational principle of the Sufi path is then given: cutting the heart's attachments to the world by turning away from the fleeting world, repenting to the hereafter, and directing all effort toward God. This requires abandoning prestige and wealth and fleeing distractions. The ultimate state is indifference to the existence or non-existence of everything. The passage then presents a detailed description of the Sufi method of dhikr (remembrance) as stated by Ghazali: seclusion in a corner, minimal worship, sitting with an empty heart and concentrated mind beginning with repeating 'Allah' on the tongue with presence of heart. This gradually leads to a stage where the word flows automatically, then to a stage where only the meaning is present in the heart without verbal forms, and then choice ceases except for maintaining the state against distractions. Finally, one waits for divine openings (futūḥ) that are granted to saints and prophets. The passage concludes that the stations of God's saints are countless. The order is: identification of the group, the method's goal, Ghazali's personal application, the general principles, and a step-by-step description of the dhikr practice.

Key Terms
كشف
معاينة
ملكوت
لوح محفوظ
خلوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think Ghazali's turn to Sufism was purely intellectual or theoretical.
Correction: The passage shows it was a practical, experiential journey involving total abandonment of worldly life.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what did the Sufis say is the way to unveiling?
A) Only knowledge
B) Only action
C) Knowledge and action
D) Seclusion

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's turn to Sufism involved a radical departure from worldly prestige through personal application of the Sufi principle of knowledge and action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What comfort, reputation, or security in your life right now are you unwilling to lose—even if holding onto it is keeping you from a deeper encounter with Allah?
?
You have read that Ghazali applied the Sufi teachings 'upon himself.' When was the last time you deliberately applied a single spiritual teaching to your own actions, not just understood it intellectually?
?
Imagine your life fell apart tonight—your status, your plans, your peace. Would you still seek God, or would you collapse into despair? What does your answer reveal about where your true trust lies?
